<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">History of East Asia</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">History of nations of eastern Asia</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Asia" title="History of Asia">History of Asia</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"East Asian History" redirects here. For the journal, see <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_History_(journal)" title="East Asian History (journal)">East Asian History (journal)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E7%90%B4%E6%A3%8B%E6%9B%B8%E7%94%BB%E5%9B%B3-The_Four_Elegant_Accomplishments_(Kin_ki_sho_ga)_MET_DP151593.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2184" data-file-width="3885" decoding="async" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/%E7%90%B4%E6%A3%8B%E6%9B%B8%E7%94%BB%E5%9B%B3-The_Four_Elegant_Accomplishments_%28Kin_ki_sho_ga%29_MET_DP151593.jpg/450px-%E7%90%B4%E6%A3%8B%E6%9B%B8%E7%94%BB%E5%9B%B3-The_Four_Elegant_Accomplishments_%28Kin_ki_sho_ga%29_MET_DP151593.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/%E7%90%B4%E6%A3%8B%E6%9B%B8%E7%94%BB%E5%9B%B3-The_Four_Elegant_Accomplishments_%28Kin_ki_sho_ga%29_MET_DP151593.jpg/675px-%E7%90%B4%E6%A3%8B%E6%9B%B8%E7%94%BB%E5%9B%B3-The_Four_Elegant_Accomplishments_%28Kin_ki_sho_ga%29_MET_DP151593.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/%E7%90%B4%E6%A3%8B%E6%9B%B8%E7%94%BB%E5%9B%B3-The_Four_Elegant_Accomplishments_%28Kin_ki_sho_ga%29_MET_DP151593.jpg/900px-%E7%90%B4%E6%A3%8B%E6%9B%B8%E7%94%BB%E5%9B%B3-The_Four_Elegant_Accomplishments_%28Kin_ki_sho_ga%29_MET_DP151593.jpg 2x" width="450"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E7%90%B4%E6%A3%8B%E6%9B%B8%E7%94%BB%E5%9B%B3-The_Four_Elegant_Accomplishments_(Kin_ki_sho_ga)_MET_DP151593.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><b>The Four Elegant Accomplishments</b></i>   (<i>kin ki sho ga</i>) by Japanese artist <a href="/wiki/Utamaro" title="Utamaro">Utamaro</a> depicts the traditional <a href="/wiki/Four_arts" title="Four arts">Four arts</a> of East Asia. From left to right, the arts depicted include the game of <a href="/wiki/Go_(game)" title="Go (game)">Go</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_painting" title="Chinese painting">Painting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_calligraphy" title="Chinese calligraphy">Calligraphy</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Guqin" title="Guqin">Guqin</a> string instrument. Produced in 1788.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_East_Asia.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="712" data-file-width="1178" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Map_of_East_Asia.png/275px-Map_of_East_Asia.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Map_of_East_Asia.png/413px-Map_of_East_Asia.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Map_of_East_Asia.png/550px-Map_of_East_Asia.png 2x" width="275"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_East_Asia.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Contemporary political map of East Asia</div></div></div>
<p>The <b>History of East Asia</b> generally encompasses the histories of <a href="/wiki/History_of_China" title="History of China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Korea" title="History of Korea">Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Taiwan" title="History of Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Japan" title="History of Japan">Japan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Mongolia" title="History of Mongolia">Mongolia</a> from prehistoric times to the present. <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a> is not a uniform term and each of its countries has a different national history, but scholars maintain that the region is also characterized by a distinct pattern of historical development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup> This is evident in the interrelationship among East Asian countries, which not only involve the sum total of historical patterns but also a specific set of patterns that has affected all or most of East Asia in successive layers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> It is also argued that East Asia and Southeast Asia form a single ethno-cultural unit, sharing history and common ethnic roots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The study of East Asian history as an <a href="/wiki/Area_studies" title="Area studies">area study</a> is a part of the rise of <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_studies" title="East Asian studies">East Asian studies</a> as an academic field in the Western World. The teaching and studying of East Asian history began in the West during the late 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>  In the United States, Asian Americans around the time of the Vietnam War believed that most history courses were <a href="/wiki/Eurocentrism" title="Eurocentrism">Eurocentric</a> and advocated for an Asian-based curriculum. At the present time, East Asian History remains a major field within Asian Studies. Nationalist historians in the region tend to stress the uniqueness of their respective country's tradition, culture, and history because it helps them legitimize their claim over territories and minimize internal disputes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-5">[5]</a></sup> There is also the case of individual authors influenced by different concepts of society and development, which lead to conflicting accounts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-5">[5]</a></sup> These, among other factors, led some scholars to stress the need for broader regional and historical frameworks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup> There have been issues with defining exact parameters for what East Asian history which as an academic study has focused on <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>'s interactions with other regions of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> It has been argued that East Asia and Southeast Asia form a single ethno-cultural area, sharing common roots and history with each other, while being distinct from other world regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>These regions, or the civilizations of China, Japan, and Korea, were under the rule of many dynasties or government systems and their boundaries changed due to inter dynasty wars on a same region or wars between regions. In prehistory, <a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo Erectus</a> lived in East and Southeast Asia from 1.8 million to 40,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMNH_Peking_Man_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMNH_Peking_Man-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many belief systems or religions which have evolved and spread in East Asia include <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_Buddhism" title="East Asian Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a>.  China was under the rule of <a href="/wiki/Xia_dynasty" title="Xia dynasty">Xia</a> (historicity disputed), <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou</a> dynasties followed by the <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han</a> dynasties. During the prehistorical period, these three regions had their own style of inter-regional politics, culture and trades, which were relatively less affected by outside world.
</p><p>Recorded civilization dates to approximately 2000 BC in China's <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang Dynasty</a> along the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_River" title="Yellow River">Yellow River Valley</a>. Civilization expanded to other areas in East Asia gradually. In Korea Gojoseon became the first organized state approximately around 195 BC. Japan emerged as a unitary state with the creation of its first constitution in 604 AD. The introduction of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> were instrumental in building East Asia's culture and economy.
</p><p>Chinese dynasties such as the <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song</a> interacted with and influenced the character of early Japan and Korea. At the turn of the first millennium AD, China was the most advanced civilization in East Asia at the time and was responsible for the <a href="/wiki/Four_Great_Inventions" title="Four Great Inventions">Four Great Inventions</a>. China's <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_China_before_1912" title="Economic history of China before 1912">GDP</a> was likely the largest in the world as well. Japan and Korea had fully coalesced as centralized states in the regimes of <a href="/wiki/Goryeo" title="Goryeo">Goryeo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heian_period" title="Heian period">Heian</a>,
</p><p>The rise of the nomadic Mongol Empire disrupted East Asia, and under the leadership of leaders such as <a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subutai" title="Subutai">Subutai</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a> brought the majority of East Asia under rule of a single state. The <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> came to rule most of modern China and all of the Korean Peninsula. The Yuan dynasty also attempted and failed to conquer Japan in <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_of_Japan" title="Mongol invasions of Japan">maritime invasions</a>. The Mongol era in East Asia was short-lived due to natural disasters and poor administrative management. In the aftermath of the Yuan dynasty's collapse, new regimes such as the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseon" title="Joseon">Joseon dynasty</a> embraced <a href="/wiki/Neo-Confucianism" title="Neo-Confucianism">Neo-Confucianism</a> as the official state ideology. Japan at this time fell into feudal civil war known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Sengoku_period" title="Sengoku period">Sengoku Jidai</a></i> which persisted for over a century and a half. At the turn of the 16th century European merchants and missionaries traveled to East Asia by sea for the first time. The Portuguese established a colony in <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau, China</a> and attempted to <a href="/wiki/Kirishitan" title="Kirishitan">Christianize Japan</a>. In the last years of the Sengoku period, Japan attempted to create a larger empire by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_invasions_of_Korea_(1592%E2%80%9398)" title="Japanese invasions of Korea (1592–98)">invading Korea</a> only being defeated by the combined forces of Korea and China in the late 16th century.
</p><p>From the 17th century onward, East Asian nations such as China, Japan, and Korea chose a policy of <a href="/wiki/Isolationism" title="Isolationism">isolationism</a> in response to European contact. The 17th and 18th centuries saw great economic and cultural growth. <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing China</a> dominated the region but <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo Japan</a> remained completely independent. At this time limited interactions with European merchants and intellectuals led to the rise of Great Britain's <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> and the beginning of Japan's <a href="/wiki/Rangaku" title="Rangaku">Dutch Studies</a>. The 1800s however saw the rise of direct European Imperialism upon the region. Qing China was unable to defend itself from various colonial expeditions from Great Britain, France and Russia during the <a href="/wiki/Opium_Wars" title="Opium Wars">Opium Wars</a>. Japan meanwhile choose the path of westernization under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meiji_period" title="Meiji period">Meiji Period</a> and attempted to modernize by following the political and economic models of Europe and the Western World. The rising <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Japanese Empire</a> forcibly <a href="/wiki/Japan%E2%80%93Korea_Treaty_of_1910" title="Japan–Korea Treaty of 1910">annexed Korea</a> in 1910. After years of civil war and decline, China's last emperor <a href="/wiki/Puyi" title="Puyi">Puyi</a> abdicated in 1912 ending China's imperial history which had persisted for over two millennium from the Qin to Qing.
</p><p>In the midst of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China_(1912%E2%80%931949)" title="Republic of China (1912–1949)">Republic of China</a>'s attempts to build a modern state, Japanese expansionism pressed onward in the first half of the twentieth century, culminating in the brutal <a href="/wiki/Second_Sino-Japanese_War" title="Second Sino-Japanese War">Second Sino-Japanese War</a> where over twenty million people died during Japan's invasion of China. Japan's wars in Asia became a part of <a href="/wiki/Pacific_War" title="Pacific War">WWII</a> after Japan's attack of the United States' Pearl Harbor. Japan's defeat in Asia by the hand of the allies contributed to the creation of a new world order under American and Soviet influence across the world. Afterwards, East Asia was caught in the cross hairs of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>. The <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">People's Republic of China</a> initially fell under the sphere of the <a href="/wiki/Second_World" title="Second World">Soviet Camp</a> but Japan under <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Japan" title="Occupation of Japan">American occupation</a> was solidly tied to <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western nations</a>. Japan's recovery became known as the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_economic_miracle" title="Japanese economic miracle">Post-war economic miracle</a>. Soviet and Western competition led to the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>, which created <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Korea" title="Division of Korea">two separate states</a> that exist in present times.
</p><p>The end of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War_(1985%E2%80%931991)" title="Cold War (1985–1991)">Cold War</a> and the rise of globalization have brought <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">People's Republic of China</a> into the world economy. Since 1980, the economies and living standards of South Korea and China have increased exponentially. In contemporary times, East Asia is a pivotal world region with a major influence on world events.  In 2010, East Asia's population made up approximately 24% of the world's population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>

<span style="font-size: 120%;">↓</span> <a href="/wiki/Future" title="Future">Future</a> <span class="nowrap">  </span></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar" style="background:#f8eaba;padding:0.4em;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Human_history" title="Template:Human history"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Human_history" title="Template talk:Human history"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Human_history&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Prehistory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Asia" title="Prehistoric Asia">Prehistoric Asia</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a> ("upright man") is believed to have lived in <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> from 1.8 million to 40,000 years ago.
</p><p>In China specifically, fossils representing 40 <i>Homo erectus</i> individuals, known as <a href="/wiki/Peking_Man" title="Peking Man">Peking Man</a>, were found near <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a> at <a href="/wiki/Zhoukoudian" title="Zhoukoudian">Zhoukoudian</a> that date to about 400,000 years ago. The species was believed to have lived for at least several hundred thousand years in China,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMNH_Peking_Man_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-AMNH_Peking_Man-8">[8]</a></sup> and possibly until 200,000 years ago in Indonesia<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Why the extra indonesia (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. They may have been the first to use fire and cook food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a> migrated into inland Asia, likely by following herds of <a href="/wiki/Bison" title="Bison">bison</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mammoth" title="Mammoth">mammoth</a> and arrived in southern <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> by about 43,000 years ago and some people moved south or east from there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
The earliest sites of <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">neolithic</a> culture include <a href="/wiki/Nanzhuangtou" title="Nanzhuangtou">Nanzhuangtou</a> culture around 9500 BC to 9000 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pengtoushan_culture" title="Pengtoushan culture">Pengtoushan culture</a> around 7500 BC to 6100 BC, <a href="/wiki/Peiligang_culture" title="Peiligang culture">Peiligang culture</a> around 7000 BC to 5000 BC. China's first villages appeared on the landscape at this time.
</p><p>In Korea the <a href="/wiki/Jeulmun_pottery_period" title="Jeulmun pottery period">Jeulmun pottery period</a> is sometimes labeled the "Korean Neolithic", but since intensive agriculture and evidence of European-style 'Neolithic' lifestyle is sparse at best, such terminology is misleading.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The Jeulmun was a period of hunting, gathering, and small-scale cultivation of plants from 20,000 BC to 8000 BC <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Archaeologists sometimes refer to this life-style pattern as 'broad-spectrum <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunting-and-gathering</a>'.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dmon_period" title="Jōmon period">Jōmon period</a> occurred in Japan from circa 14,000 BC to 300BC, with some characteristics of both Neolithic and <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a> culture.
</p>

Followed by <a href="/wiki/Post-classical_history" title="Post-classical history">Post-classical History</a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Ancient_history" title="Template:Ancient history"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Ancient_history" title="Template talk:Ancient history"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Ancient_history&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Chinese_dynasties">Ancient Chinese dynasties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Ancient Chinese dynasties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Xia_dynasty" title="Xia dynasty">Xia dynasty</a> of China (from c. 2100 to c. 1600 BC) is the first dynasty to be described in ancient historical records such as <a href="/wiki/Sima_Qian" title="Sima Qian">Sima Qian</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Records_of_the_Grand_Historian" title="Records of the Grand Historian">Records of the Grand Historian</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Bamboo_Annals" title="Bamboo Annals">Bamboo Annals</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-state1_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-state1-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Ancient_Dynasties_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Ancient_Dynasties-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following this was the <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a>, which ruled in the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_River" title="Yellow River">Yellow River</a> valley. The classic account of the Shang comes from texts such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Documents" title="Book of Documents">Book of Documents</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Bamboo_Annals" title="Bamboo Annals">Bamboo Annals</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Records_of_the_Grand_Historian" title="Records of the Grand Historian">Records of the Grand Historian</a></i>.  According to the traditional chronology, the Shang ruled from 1766 BC to 1122 BC, but according to the chronology based upon the "current text" of <i>Bamboo Annals</i>, they ruled from 1556 BC to 1046 BC.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou dynasty</a> of <span title="circa">c.</span><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1046</span>–256 BC lasted longer than any other dynasty in <a href="/wiki/History_of_China" title="History of China">Chinese history</a>. However, the actual political and military control of China by the dynasty, surnamed <a href="/wiki/Ji_(surname)" title="Ji (surname)">Ji</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">姬</span>), lasted only until 771 BC, a period known as the <a href="/wiki/Western_Zhou" title="Western Zhou">Western Zhou</a>. This period of Chinese history produced what many consider the zenith of Chinese <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a>-ware making. The dynasty also spans the period in which the written script evolved into its modern form with the use of an archaic clerical script that emerged during the late <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomads_on_the_Mongolian_Steppe">Nomads on the Mongolian Steppe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Nomads on the Mongolian Steppe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The territories of modern-day <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inner_Mongolia" title="Inner Mongolia">Inner Mongolia</a> in ancient times was inhabited by nomadic tribes. The cultures and languages in these areas were fluid and changed frequently. The use of horses to herd and move started during the Iron Age.  A large area of Mongolia was under the influence of <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic peoples</a>, while the southwestern part of Mongolia was mostly under the influence of <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European peoples</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Tocharians" title="Tocharians">Tocharians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythian</a> tribes. In antiquity, the eastern portions of both <a href="/wiki/Inner_Mongolia" title="Inner Mongolia">Inner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Outer_Mongolia" title="Outer Mongolia">Outer</a> Mongolia were inhabited by Mongolic peoples descended from the <a href="/wiki/Donghu_people" title="Donghu people">Donghu people</a> and numerous other tribes These were <a href="/wiki/Tengrism" title="Tengrism">Tengriist</a> horse-riding pastoralist kingdoms that had close contact with the agrarian Chinese. As a nomadic confederation composed of various clans the Donghu were prosperous in the 4th century BC, forcing surrounding tribes to pay tribute and constantly harassing the Chinese <a href="/wiki/Zhao_(state)" title="Zhao (state)">State of Zhao</a> (325 BC, during the early years of the reign of <a href="/wiki/King_Wuling_of_Zhao" title="King Wuling of Zhao">Wuling</a>). To appease the nomads local Chinese rulers often gave important hostages and arranged marriages.  In 208 BC Xiongnu emperor <a href="/wiki/Modu_Chanyu" title="Modu Chanyu">Modu Chanyu</a>, in his first major military campaign, defeated the <a href="/wiki/Donghu_people" title="Donghu people">Donghu</a>, who split into the new tribes <a href="/wiki/Xianbei" title="Xianbei">Xianbei</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wuhuan" title="Wuhuan">Wuhuan</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Xiongnu" title="Xiongnu">Xiongnu</a> were the largest nomadic enemies of the Han Dynasty fighting <a href="/wiki/Han%E2%80%93Xiongnu_War" title="Han–Xiongnu War">wars</a> for over three centuries with the Han Dynasty before dissolving. Afterwards the Xianbei returned to rule the Steppe north of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Wall_of_China" title="Great Wall of China">Great Wall</a>. The titles of Khangan and <a href="/wiki/Khan_(title)" title="Khan (title)">Khan</a> originated from the <a href="/wiki/Xianbei" title="Xianbei">Xianbei</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Korea">Ancient Korea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Ancient Korea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Memorabilia_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Memorabilia of the Three Kingdoms">Memorabilia of the Three Kingdoms</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Gojoseon" title="Gojoseon">Gojoseon</a> was established in 2333 BC by <a href="/wiki/Dangun" title="Dangun">Dangun</a>, who was said to be the offspring of a heavenly prince and a bear-woman. Gojoseon fostered an independent culture in <a href="/wiki/Liaoning" title="Liaoning">Liaoning</a> and along the <a href="/wiki/Taedong_River" title="Taedong River">Taedong River</a>. In 108 BC, the Chinese <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> under <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wu_of_Han" title="Emperor Wu of Han">Emperor Wu</a> invaded and conquered Gojoseon. The Han established four commanderies to administer the former Gojoseon territory. After the fragmentation of the Han Empire during the 3rd century and the subsequent chaotic 4th century, the area was lost from the Chinese was reconquered by the Empire of <a href="/wiki/Goguryeo" title="Goguryeo">Goguryeo</a> in 313 AD.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Three_Kingdoms_of_Korea_Map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="827" data-file-width="658" decoding="async" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Three_Kingdoms_of_Korea_Map.png/170px-Three_Kingdoms_of_Korea_Map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Three_Kingdoms_of_Korea_Map.png/255px-Three_Kingdoms_of_Korea_Map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Three_Kingdoms_of_Korea_Map.png/340px-Three_Kingdoms_of_Korea_Map.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Three_Kingdoms_of_Korea_Map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms_of_Korea" title="Three Kingdoms of Korea">Three Kingdoms of Korea</a>, at the end of the 5th century</div></div></div>
<p>In 58 BC, the Korean Peninsula was divided into three kingdoms, <a href="/wiki/Baekje" title="Baekje">Baekje</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silla" title="Silla">Silla</a> and <a href="/wiki/Goguryeo" title="Goguryeo">Goguryeo</a>. Although they shared a similar language and culture, these three kingdoms constantly fought with each other for control of the peninsula. Furthermore, Goguryeo had been engaged in constant wars with the Chinese. This included the <a href="/wiki/Goguryeo%E2%80%93Sui_War" title="Goguryeo–Sui War">Goguryeo–Sui War</a>, where the Kingdom of Goguryeo managed to repel the invading forces of the <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui dynasty</a>.
</p><p>As the Kingdom of Silla conquered nearby city-states, they gained access to the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Sea" title="Yellow Sea">Yellow Sea</a>, making direct contact with the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> possible. The Tang dynasty teamed up with Silla and formed a strategy to invade Goguryeo. Since Goguryeo had been able to repel earlier Chinese invasions from the North, perhaps Gorguryeo would fall if it were attacked by Silla from the south at the same time. However, in order to do this, the Tang-Silla alliance had to eliminate Goguryeo's nominal ally Baekje and secure a base of operations in southern Korea for a second front. In 660, the coalition troops of Silla and Tang of China attacked Baekje, resulting in the annexation of Baekje by Silla. Together, Silla and Tang effectively eliminated Baekje when they captured the capital of Sabi, as well as Baekje's last king, Uija, and most of the royal family.
However, <a href="/wiki/Yamato_period" title="Yamato period">Yamato</a> Japan and Baekje had been long-standing and very close allies. In 663, Baekje revival forces and a Japanese naval fleet convened in southern Baekje to confront the Silla forces in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Baekgang" title="Battle of Baekgang">Battle of Baekgang</a>. The Tang dynasty also sent 7,000 soldiers and 170 ships. After five naval confrontations that took place in August 663 at Baekgang, considered the lower reaches of Tongjin river, the Silla–Tang forces emerged victorious.
</p><p>The Silla–Tang forces turned their attention to Goguryeo. Although Goguryeo had repelled the Sui Dynasty a century earlier, attacks by the Tang Dynasty from the west proved too formidable. The Silla–Tang alliance emerged victorious in the <a href="/wiki/Goguryeo%E2%80%93Tang_War" title="Goguryeo–Tang War">Goguryeo–Tang War</a>. Silla thus unified most of the Korean Peninsula in 668. The kingdom's reliance on China's Tang dynasty had its price. Silla had to forcibly resist the imposition of Chinese rule over the entire peninsula. Silla then fought for nearly a decade to expel Chinese forces to finally establish a unified kingdom as far north as modern <a href="/wiki/Pyongyang" title="Pyongyang">Pyongyang</a>. 
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Japan">Early Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Early Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Japan was inhabited more than 30,000 years ago, when land bridges connected Japan to Korea and China to the south and Siberia to the north. With rising sea levels, the 4 major islands took form around 20,000 years ago, and the lands connecting today's Japan to the continental Asia completely disappeared 15,000 ~ 10,000 years ago. Thereafter, some migrations continued by way of the Korean peninsula, which would serve as Japan's main avenue for cultural exchange with the continental Asia until the medieval period. The mythology of ancient Japan is contained within the <a href="/wiki/Kojiki" title="Kojiki">Kojiki</a> ('Records of Ancient Matters') which describes the creation myth of Japan and its lineage of Emperors to the Sun Goddess <a href="/wiki/Amaterasu" title="Amaterasu">Amaterasu</a>.
</p><p>Ancient <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">pottery</a> has been uncovered in Japan, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Kyushu" title="Kyushu">Kyushu</a>, that points to two major periods: the <a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dmon_period" title="Jōmon period">Jōmon</a> (c. 7,500–250 BC, 縄文時代 <i>Jōmon Jidai</i> ) and the <a href="/wiki/Yayoi_period" title="Yayoi period">Yayoi</a> (c. 250 BC – 250 AD, 弥生時代 <i>Yayoi Jidai</i>). <i>Jōmon</i> can be translated as 'cord marks' and refers to the pattern on the pottery of the time; this style was more ornate than the later Yayoi type, which has been found at more widespread sites (e.g. around Tokyo) and seems to have been developed for more practical purposes.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth_of_Confucianism_and_Taoism">Birth of Confucianism and Taoism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Birth of Confucianism and Taoism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a></div>
<p>Confucianism and Taoism originated in the <a href="/wiki/Spring_and_Autumn_period" title="Spring and Autumn period">Spring and Autumn period</a>, arising from the historic figures of <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Laozi" title="Laozi">Laozi</a>. They have functioned has both competing and complementary belief systems. Confucianism emphasizes social order and filial piety while Taoism emphasizes the universal force of the <a href="/wiki/Tao" title="Tao">Tao</a> and spiritual well being.
</p><p>Confucianism is an <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophical</a> system that developed during the <a href="/wiki/Spring_and_Autumn_period" title="Spring and Autumn period">Spring and Autumn period</a>. It later developed <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmological</a> elements in the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-craig_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-craig-18">[18]</a></sup> Following the official abandonment of <a href="/wiki/Legalism_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Legalism (Chinese philosophy)">Legalism</a> in China after the <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a>, Confucianism became the official state ideology of the Han. Nonetheless, from the Han period onwards, most Chinese emperors have used a mix of <a href="/wiki/Legalism_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Legalism (Chinese philosophy)">Legalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a> as their ruling doctrine. The disintegration of the Han in the second century CE opened the way for the <a href="/wiki/Soteriology" title="Soteriology">soteriological</a> doctrines of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a> to dominate intellectual life at that time.
</p><p>A Confucian revival began during the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a>. In the late Tang, Confucianism developed aspects on the model of Buddhism and Taoism that gradually evolved into what is now known as <a href="/wiki/Neo-Confucianism" title="Neo-Confucianism">Neo-Confucianism</a>. This reinvigorated form was adopted as the basis of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_exams" title="Imperial exams">imperial exams</a> and the core philosophy of the <a href="/wiki/Scholar-official" title="Scholar-official">scholar-official class</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a>. Confucianism would reign supreme as an ideology influencing all of East Asia until the fall of the Qing Dynasty in 1911.
</p><p>Taoism as a movement originates from the semi mystical figure of Laozi, who allegedly lived during the 6th–5th century BC. His teachings revolved around personal serenity, balance in the universe and the life source of the <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Tao</a>. The first organized form of Taoism, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tianshi_Dao" title="Tianshi Dao">Tianshi</a> (Celestial Masters') school (later known as <a href="/wiki/Zhengyi_Dao" title="Zhengyi Dao">Zhengyi</a> school), developed from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_Pecks_of_Rice" title="Five Pecks of Rice">Five Pecks of Rice</a> movement at the end of the 2nd century CE; the latter had been founded by <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Daoling" title="Zhang Daoling">Zhang Daoling</a>, who claimed that Laozi appeared to him in the year 142.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The Tianshi school was officially recognized by ruler <a href="/wiki/Cao_Cao" title="Cao Cao">Cao Cao</a> in 215, legitimizing Cao Cao's rise to power in return.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Laozi received imperial recognition as a divinity in the mid-2nd century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Taoism, in form of the <a href="/wiki/Shangqing_School" title="Shangqing School">Shangqing School</a>, gained official status in China again during the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> (618–907), whose emperors claimed <a href="/wiki/Laozi" title="Laozi">Laozi</a> as their relative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The Shangqing movement, however, had developed much earlier, in the 4th century, on the basis of a series of revelations by gods and spirits to a certain Yang Xi in the years between 364 and 370.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Qin_and_Han_dynasties">Qin and Han dynasties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Qin and Han dynasties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a></div>
<p>In 221 BC, the state of Qin succeeded in conquering the other six states, creating the first imperial dynasty of China for the first time. Following the death of the emperor Qin Shi Huang, the Qin dynasty collapsed and control was taken over by the Han dynasty in 206 BC. In 220 AD, the Han empire collapsed into the <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms" title="Three Kingdoms">Three Kingdoms</a>. The series of trade routes known as <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> began during the Han dynasty.
</p><p>Qin Shi Huang ruled the unified China directly with absolute power. In contrast to the decentralized and feudal rule of earlier dynasties the Qin set up a number of 'commanderries' around the country which answered directly to the emperor. Nationwide the political philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Legalism_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Legalism (Chinese philosophy)">Legalism</a> was used as a means of the statecraft and writings promoting rival ideas such as Confucianism were prohibited or controlled. In his reign China created the first continuous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Wall" title="Great Wall">Great Wall</a> with the use of forced labor and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qin_campaign_against_the_Yue_tribes" title="Qin campaign against the Yue tribes">Invasions</a> were launched southward to annex Vietnam. Eventually, rebels rose against the Qin's brutal reign and fought civil wars for control of China. Ultimately the Han dynasty arose and ruled China for over four centuries in what accounted for a long period in prosperity, with a brief interruption by the <a href="/wiki/Xin_dynasty" title="Xin dynasty">Xin dynasty</a>. The Han dynasty fought constant wars with nomadic Xiongnu for centuries before finally dissolving the tribe.
</p><p>The Han dynasty played a great role in developing the Silk Road which would transfer wealth and ideas across Eurasia for millennia, and also invented paper. Though the Han enjoyed great military and economic success it was strained by the rise of aristocrats who disobeyed the central government. Public frustration provoked the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Turban_Rebellion" title="Yellow Turban Rebellion">Yellow Turban Rebellion</a> – though a failure it nonetheless accelerated the empire's downfall. After 208 AD the Han dynasty broke up into <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms" title="Three Kingdoms">rival kingdoms</a>. China would remain divided until 581 under the Sui dynasty, during the era of division Buddhism would be introduced to China for the first time.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Era_of_disunion_in_China">Era of disunion in China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Era of disunion in China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms" title="Three Kingdoms">Three Kingdoms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sixteen_Kingdoms" title="Sixteen Kingdoms">Sixteen Kingdoms</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Northern_and_Southern_dynasties" title="Northern and Southern dynasties">Northern and Southern dynasties</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms" title="Three Kingdoms">Three Kingdoms</a> period consisted of the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Cao_Wei" title="Cao Wei">Wei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shu_Han" title="Shu Han">Shu</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Wu" title="Eastern Wu">Wu</a>. It began when the ruler of Wei, <a href="/wiki/Cao_Cao" title="Cao Cao">Cao Cao</a>, was defeated by <a href="/wiki/Liu_Bei" title="Liu Bei">Liu Bei</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sun_Quan" title="Sun Quan">Sun Quan</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Red_Cliffs" title="Battle of Red Cliffs">Battle of Red Cliffs</a>. After Cao Cao's death in AD 220, his son Cao Pi became emperor of Wei. Liu Bei and Sun Quan declared themselves emperor of Shu and Wu respectively. Many famous personages in Chinese history were born during this period, including <a href="/wiki/Hua_Tuo" title="Hua Tuo">Hua Tuo</a> and the great military strategist <a href="/wiki/Zhuge_Liang" title="Zhuge Liang">Zhuge Liang</a>. <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, which was introduced during the Han dynasty, also became popular in this period. Two years after <a href="/wiki/Conquest_of_Shu_by_Wei" title="Conquest of Shu by Wei">Wei conquered Shu</a> in AD 263, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sima_Yan" title="Sima Yan">Sima Yan</a>, Wei's Imperial Chancellor, overthrew Wei and started the <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(266%E2%80%93420)" title="Jin dynasty (266–420)">Western Jin dynasty</a>. The conquest of Wu by the Western Jin dynasty ended the Three Kingdoms period, and China was unified again. However, the Western Jin did not last long. Following the death of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wu_of_Jin" title="Emperor Wu of Jin">Sima Yan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Eight_Princes" title="War of the Eight Princes">War of the Eight Princes</a> began. This war weakened the Jin dynasty, and it soon fell to the kingdom of Han Zhao. This ushered in the <a href="/wiki/Sixteen_Kingdoms" title="Sixteen Kingdoms">Sixteen Kingdoms</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Northern_Wei" title="Northern Wei">Northern Wei</a> was established by the <a href="/wiki/Tuoba" title="Tuoba">Tuoba</a> clan of the <a href="/wiki/Xianbei" title="Xianbei">Xianbei</a> people in AD 386, when they united the northern part of China. During the Northern Wei, Buddhism flourished, and became an important tool for the emperors of the Northern Wei, since they were believed to be living <a href="/wiki/Incarnation" title="Incarnation">incarnations</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddha" title="Buddha">Buddha</a>. Soon, the Northern Wei was divided into the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Wei" title="Eastern Wei">Eastern Wei</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_Wei" title="Western Wei">Western Wei</a>. These were followed by the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Zhou" title="Northern Zhou">Northern Zhou</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Qi" title="Northern Qi">Northern Qi</a>. In the south, the dynasties were much less stable than the Northern dynasties. The four Southern dynasties were weakened by conflicts between the ruling families.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spread_of_Buddhism">Spread of Buddhism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Spread of Buddhism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Buddhism, also one of the major religions in East Asia, was introduced into China during the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> from <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a> in the 1st century BC. Buddhism was originally introduced to <a href="/wiki/Korean_Buddhism" title="Korean Buddhism">Korea</a> from China in 372, and eventually arrived in Japan around the turn of the 6th century.
</p><p>For a long time Buddhism remained a foreign religion with a few believers in China. During the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a>, a fair amount of translations from Sanskrit into Chinese were done by Chinese priests, and Buddhism became one of the major religions of the Chinese along with the other two indigenous religions. In Korea, Buddhism was not seen to conflict with the rites of nature worship; it was allowed to blend in with Shamanism. Thus, the mountains that were believed to be the residence of spirits in pre-Buddhist times became the sites of Buddhist temples. Though Buddhism initially enjoyed wide acceptance, even being supported as the state ideology during the <a href="/wiki/Goguryeo" title="Goguryeo">Goguryeo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silla" title="Silla">Silla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baekje" title="Baekje">Baekje</a>, <a href="/wiki/Balhae" title="Balhae">Balhae</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Goryeo" title="Goryeo">Goryeo</a> periods, Buddhism in Korea suffered extreme repression during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseon_dynasty" title="Joseon dynasty">Joseon dynasty</a>.
</p><p>In Japan, Buddhism and <a href="/wiki/Shinto" title="Shinto">Shinto</a> were combined using the theological theory "Ryōbushintō", which says Shinto deities are avatars of various Buddhist entities, including Buddhas and Bodhisattvas (<a href="/wiki/Shinbutsu-sh%C5%ABg%C5%8D" title="Shinbutsu-shūgō">Shinbutsu-shūgō</a>). This became the mainstream notion of Japanese religion. In fact until the Meiji government declared their separation in the mid-19th century, many Japanese people believed that Buddhism and Shinto were one religion.
</p><p>In Mongolia, Buddhism flourished two times; first in the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> (13th–14th centuries), and finally in the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a> (16th–19th centuries) from Tibet in the last 2000 years. It was mixed in with <a href="/wiki/Tengrism" title="Tengrism">Tengeriism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_shamanism" title="Mongolian shamanism">Shamanism</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sui_dynasty">Sui dynasty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Sui dynasty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui dynasty</a></div>
<p>In AD 581, Yang Jian overthrew the Northern Zhou, and established the <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui dynasty</a>. Later, Yang Jian, who became <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wen_of_Sui" title="Emperor Wen of Sui">Sui Wendi</a>, conquered the <a href="/wiki/Chen_dynasty" title="Chen dynasty">Chen dynasty</a>, and united China. However, this dynasty was short-lived. Sui Wendi's successor, <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Yang_of_Sui" title="Emperor Yang of Sui">Sui Yangdi</a>, expanded the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Canal_(China)" title="Grand Canal (China)">Grand Canal</a>, and launched <a href="/wiki/Goguryeo%E2%80%93Sui_War" title="Goguryeo–Sui War">four disastrous wars</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Goguryeo" title="Goguryeo">Goguryeo</a>. These projects depleted the resources and the workforce of the Sui. In AD 618, Sui Yangdi was murdered. <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Gaozu_of_Tang" title="Emperor Gaozu of Tang">Li Yuan</a>, the former governor of <a href="/wiki/Taiyuan" title="Taiyuan">Taiyuan</a>, declared himself the emperor, and founded the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spread_of_Civil_Service">Spread of Civil Service</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Spread of Civil Service">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A government system supported by a large class of Confucian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scholar-bureaucrats" title="Scholar-bureaucrats">literati</a> selected through civil service examinations was perfected under Tang rule. This competitive procedure was designed to draw the best talents into government. But perhaps an even greater consideration for the Tang rulers, aware that imperial dependence on powerful aristocratic families and warlords would have destabilizing consequences, was to create a body of career officials having no autonomous territorial or functional power base. As it turned out, these scholar-officials acquired status in their local communities, family ties, and shared values that connected them to the imperial court. From Tang times until the closing days of the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a> in 1911–1912, scholar officials often functioned often as between the grassroots level and the government. This model of government had an influence on Japan and Korea.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_History">Medieval History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Medieval History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Goryeo">Goryeo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Goryeo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Silla slowly began to decline and the power vacuum this created led to several rebellious states rising up and taking on the old historical names of Korea's ancient kingdoms. <a href="/wiki/Gyeon_Hwon" title="Gyeon Hwon">Gyeon Hwon</a>, a peasant leader and Silla army officer, taking over the old territory of Baekje and declared himself the king of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubaekje" title="Hubaekje">Hubaekje</a> ("later Baekje"). Meanwhile, an aristocratic Buddhist monk leader, <a href="/wiki/Gung_Ye" title="Gung Ye">Gung Ye</a>, declared a new Goguryeo state in the north, known as Later Goguryeo (Hugoguryo). There then followed a protracted power struggle for control of the peninsula.
</p><p>Gung Ye began to refer to himself as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddha" title="Buddha">Buddha</a>, began to persecute people who expressed their opposition against his religious arguments. He executed many monks, then later even his own wife and two sons, and the public began to turn away from him. His costly rituals and harsh rule caused even more opposition. He also moved the capital in 905, changed the name of his kingdom to Majin in 904 then <a href="/wiki/Taebong" title="Taebong">Taebong</a> in 911. In 918, Gung Ye was deposed by his own generals, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wang_Geon" title="Wang Geon">Wang Geon</a>, the previous chief minister was raised to the throne. Gung Ye is said to have escaped the palace, but was killed shortly thereafter either by a soldier or by peasants who mistook him for a thief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Wang Geon, who would posthumously be known by his temple name of <a href="/wiki/Taejo_of_Goryeo" title="Taejo of Goryeo">Taejo of Goryeo</a>.
</p><p>Soon thereafter, the <a href="/wiki/Goryeo" title="Goryeo">Goryeo</a> dynasty was proclaimed, and Taejo went on to defeat the rivaling Silla and Hubaekje to reunite the three kingdoms in 936.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Following the destruction of Balhae by the <a href="/wiki/Khitan_people" title="Khitan people">Khitan</a> <a href="/wiki/Liao_dynasty" title="Liao dynasty">Liao dynasty</a> in 927, the last crown prince of Balhae and much of the ruling class sought refuge in Goryeo, where they were warmly welcomed and given land by Taejo. In addition, Taejo included the Balhae crown prince in the Goryeo royal family, unifying the two successor states of Goguryeo and, according to Korean historians, achieving a "true national unification" of Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKim2012120_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKim2012120-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee1984103_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee1984103-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mongol_Empire_and_Yuan_dynasty">Mongol Empire and Yuan dynasty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Mongol Empire and Yuan dynasty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44"
