<b>needs expansion</b>. You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">May 2008</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a></div>
<p>In the early 13th century Genghis Khan united warring Mongol tribes into the united <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> in 1206. The Mongols would proceed to conquer the majority of modern East Asia. Meanwhile, China were divided into five competing states. From 1211, Mongol forces invaded <a href="/wiki/North_China" title="North China">North China</a>. In 1227 the Mongol Empire conquered <a href="/wiki/Western_Xia" title="Western Xia">Western Xia</a>. In 1234, <a href="/wiki/%C3%96gedei_Khan" title="Ögedei Khan">Ogedei Khan</a> extinguished the <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(1115%E2%80%931234)" title="Jin dynasty (1115–1234)">Jin dynasty</a>.
</p><p>The northern part of China was annexed by Mongol Empire. In 1231, the Mongols began to invade Korea, and quickly captured all the territory of the <a href="/wiki/Goryeo" title="Goryeo">Goryeo</a> outside the southernmost tip. The Goryeo royal family retreated to the sea outside the city of Seoul to <a href="/wiki/Ganghwa_Island" title="Ganghwa Island">Ganghwa Island</a>. Goryeo was divided between collaborators and resisters to the invaders. However, at the time, the Goryeo Sannotei on the peninsula resisted until 1275.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mongols-map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="525" data-file-width="728" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Mongols-map.png/220px-Mongols-map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Mongols-map.png/330px-Mongols-map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Mongols-map.png/440px-Mongols-map.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mongols-map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The boundary of 13th century <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> and location of today's <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongols</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the 1250s, the Mongols invaded the last remaining state in southern China – the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Song" title="Southern Song">Southern Song</a>. The invasion carried on for over thirty years, and likely resulted in millions of casualties. In 1271, <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a> proclaimed the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> of China in the traditional Chinese style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The last remnants of the Song were defeated at sea in 1279. China was unified under the Yuan dynasty. Kublai Khan and his administration shifted to the <a href="/wiki/Zhongyuan" title="Zhongyuan">Central Plains</a> area and embraced <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a>.
</p><p>By 1275, Goryeo had surrendered to the Yuan dynasty as a vassal. Members of the Goryeo royal family were raised to understand Mongol culture, and intermarried with the Yuan imperial family.
</p><p>Japan was seriously threatened by the Yuan forces from the East Asian mainland. In 1274, Kublai Khan appointed <a href="/wiki/Yudu_County" title="Yudu County">Yudu</a>. In order to recruit Marshal Dongdu to command the Yuan forces, Han Bing and the Goryeo army began the first expedition to Japan. The Yuan dynasty invaded Japan in two separate invasions, both of which were disrupted by natural typhoons. These two invasions both occupied the town of <a href="/wiki/Kitakyushu" title="Kitakyushu">Kitakyushu</a> before being swept into the sea. At the time the Yuan dynasty fleet was the largest fleet in the history of the world.
</p><p>In order to cope with the nationwide mobilization of the powerful Yuan army, Japan's economy and military were placed under severe pressure. The Japanese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kamakura_Shogunate" title="Kamakura Shogunate">Kamakura Shogunate</a> had difficulty compensating its soldiers who had defended the country, which intensified the contradiction between the domestic warrior groups. The ruling system collapsed in the first half of the 14th century<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Evidence??? (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_and_Technology">Science and Technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Science and Technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gunpowder">Gunpowder</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Gunpowder">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_gunpowder" title="History of gunpowder">History of gunpowder</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba_Mongol_Invasion_Takezaki_Suenaga_2_Page_5-7.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3176" data-file-width="10000" decoding="async" height="92" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba_Mongol_Invasion_Takezaki_Suenaga_2_Page_5-7.jpg/290px-M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba_Mongol_Invasion_Takezaki_Suenaga_2_Page_5-7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba_Mongol_Invasion_Takezaki_Suenaga_2_Page_5-7.jpg/435px-M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba_Mongol_Invasion_Takezaki_Suenaga_2_Page_5-7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba_Mongol_Invasion_Takezaki_Suenaga_2_Page_5-7.jpg/580px-M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba_Mongol_Invasion_Takezaki_Suenaga_2_Page_5-7.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba_Mongol_Invasion_Takezaki_Suenaga_2_Page_5-7.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Mongol bomb thrown against a charging Japanese <a href="/wiki/Samurai" title="Samurai">samurai</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_of_Japan" title="Mongol invasions of Japan">Mongol invasions of Japan</a>, 1281.</div></div></div><p> Most sources credit the discovery of gunpowder to Chinese <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemists</a> in the 9th century searching for an <a href="/wiki/Elixir_of_life" title="Elixir of life">elixir of immortality</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The discovery of gunpowder was probably the product of centuries of alchemical experimentation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chase_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-chase-30">[30]</a></sup> Saltpetre was known to the Chinese by the mid-1st century AD and there is strong evidence of the use of saltpetre and sulfur in various largely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medicinal" title="Medicinal">medicine</a> combinations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> A Chinese alchemical text from 492 noted that saltpeter gave off a purple flame when ignited, providing for the first time a practical and reliable means of distinguishing it from other inorganic salts, making it possible to evaluate and compare purification techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chase_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-chase-30">[30]</a></sup> By most accounts, the earliest <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> descriptions of the purification of saltpeter do not appear until the 13th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chase_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-chase-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kelly23_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-kelly23-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first reference to gunpowder is probably a passage in the <i>Zhenyuan miaodao yaolüe</i>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taoist" title="Taoist">Taoism</a> text tentatively dated to the mid-9th century:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chase_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-chase-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>Some have heated together <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Realgar" title="Realgar">realgar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potassium_nitrate" title="Potassium nitrate">saltpeter</a> with <a href="/wiki/Honey" title="Honey">honey</a>; smoke and flames result, so that their hands and faces have been burnt, and even the whole house where they were working burned down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jiao_zi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="537" data-file-width="308" decoding="async" height="262" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Jiao_zi.jpg/150px-Jiao_zi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Jiao_zi.jpg/225px-Jiao_zi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Jiao_zi.jpg/300px-Jiao_zi.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jiao_zi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jiaozi_(currency)" title="Jiaozi (currency)">Jiaozi (currency)</a>, 11th century paper-printed <a href="/wiki/Banknote" title="Banknote">money</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Song_Dynasty" title="Song Dynasty">Song Dynasty</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The earliest surviving recipes for gunpowder can be found in the Chinese <a href="/wiki/Military" title="Military">military</a> treatise <i><a href="/wiki/Wujing_Zongyao" title="Wujing Zongyao">Wujing zongyao</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chase_30-4"><a href="#cite_note-chase-30">[30]</a></sup> of 1044 AD, which contains three: two for use in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Incendiary_bomb" title="Incendiary bomb">incendiary bombs</a> to be thrown by <a href="/wiki/Siege_engine" title="Siege engine">siege engines</a> and one intended as fuel for poison <a href="/wiki/Smoke_bomb" title="Smoke bomb">smoke bombs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The formulas in the <i>Wujing zongyao</i> range from 27 to 50 percent nitrate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_volume_5_part_7_345_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_volume_5_part_7_345-35">[35]</a></sup> Experimenting with different levels of saltpetre content eventually produced <a href="/wiki/Bomb" title="Bomb">bombs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grenade" title="Grenade">grenades</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Land_mine" title="Land mine">land mines</a>, in addition to giving <a href="/wiki/Fire_arrow" title="Fire arrow">fire arrows</a> a new lease on life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chase_30-5"><a href="#cite_note-chase-30">[30]</a></sup> By the end of the 12th century, there were <a href="/wiki/Cast_iron" title="Cast iron">cast iron</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grenades" title="Grenades">grenades</a> filled with gunpowder formulations capable of bursting through their metal containers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_volume_5_part_7_347_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_volume_5_part_7_347-36">[36]</a></sup> The 14th century <i><a href="/wiki/Huolongjing" title="Huolongjing">Huolongjing</a></i> contains gunpowder recipes with nitrate levels ranging from 12 to 91 percent, six of which approach the theoretical composition for maximal explosive force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_volume_5_part_7_345_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-needham_volume_5_part_7_345-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>In China, the 13th century saw the beginnings of <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">rocketry</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> and the manufacture of the oldest gun still in existence,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chase_30-6"><a href="#cite_note-chase-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> a descendant of the earlier <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fire-lance" title="Fire-lance">fire-lance</a>, a gunpowder-fueled <a href="/wiki/Flamethrower" title="Flamethrower">flamethrower</a> that could shoot shrapnel along with fire. The <i>Huolongjing</i> text of the 14th century also describes hollow, gunpowder-packed <a href="/wiki/Shell_(projectile)#Early_shells" title="Shell (projectile)">exploding cannonballs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_volume_5_part_7_264_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_volume_5_part_7_264-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 13th century contemporary documentation shows gunpowder beginning to spread from China by the <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongols</a> to the rest of the world, starting with Europe<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kelly23_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-kelly23-32">[32]</a></sup> and the Islamic world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urbanski_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-urbanski-41">[41]</a></sup> The Arabs acquired knowledge of saltpetre – which they called "Chinese snow" (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">ثلج الصين</span> <span title="DIN 31635 Arabic (Arabic language) transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">thalj al-ṣīn</i></span>) – around 1240 and, soon afterward, of gunpowder; they also learned of fireworks ("Chinese flowers") and rockets ("Chinese arrows").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urbanski_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-urbanski-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_volume_5_part_7_108_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_volume_5_part_7_108-42">[42]</a></sup> Persians called saltpeter "Chinese salt"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> or "salt from Chinese salt marshes" (<span title="DIN 31635 Arabic (Persian language) transliteration"><i lang="fa-Latn">namak shūra chīnī</i></span> <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="fa">نمک شوره چيني</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Historian Ahmad Y. al-Hassan argues – <i>contra</i> the general notion – that the Chinese technology passed through Arabic alchemy and chemistry before the 13th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Gunpowder arrived in India by the mid-14th century, but could have been introduced by the <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongols</a> perhaps as early as the mid-13th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chase130_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-chase130-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Printing">Printing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Printing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_typography_in_East_Asia" title="History of typography in East Asia">History of typography in East Asia</a></div>
<p>The first known <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_typography_in_East_Asia" title="History of typography in East Asia">movable type system</a> was invented in China around 1040 AD by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pi_Sheng" title="Pi Sheng">Pi Sheng</a> (990–1051) (spelled Bi Sheng in the <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">Pinyin</a> system).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_volume_5_part_1_201_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_volume_5_part_1_201-52">[52]</a></sup> Pi Sheng's type was made of baked clay, as described by the Chinese scholar <a href="/wiki/Shen_Kuo" title="Shen Kuo">Shen Kuo</a> (1031–1095). The world's first metal-based <a href="/wiki/Movable_type" title="Movable type">movable type</a> was invented in <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a> in 1234, 210 years before <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Gutenberg" title="Johannes Gutenberg">Johannes Gutenberg</a> invented the printing press in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>. <a href="/wiki/Jikji" title="Jikji">Jikji</a> is the world's oldest extant movable metal print book. It was published in Heungdeok Temple in 1377, 78 years prior to Gutenberg's <a href="/wiki/Gutenberg_Bible" title="Gutenberg Bible">"42-Line Bible"</a> printed during the years 1452–1455.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Modern_History">Early Modern History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Early Modern History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Ming_dynasty:_1368.E2.80.931644"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ming_dynasty:_1368–1644">Ming dynasty: 1368–1644</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Ming dynasty: 1368–1644">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a></div>
<p>The Ming period is the only era of later imperial history during which all of <a href="/wiki/China_proper" title="China proper">China proper</a> was ruled by <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">ethnic Han</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ming_Empire_cca_1580_(en).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2344" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Ming_Empire_cca_1580_%28en%29.svg/220px-Ming_Empire_cca_1580_%28en%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Ming_Empire_cca_1580_%28en%29.svg/330px-Ming_Empire_cca_1580_%28en%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Ming_Empire_cca_1580_%28en%29.svg/440px-Ming_Empire_cca_1580_%28en%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ming_Empire_cca_1580_(en).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ming China around 1580</div></div></div>
<p>All the counties in China had a county government, a Confucian school, and the standard Chinese family system. Typically the dominant local elite consisted of high status families composed of the gentry owners and managers of land and of other forms of wealth, as well as smaller groups that were subject to elite domination and protection. Much attention was paid to genealogy to prove that high status was inherited from generations back.  Substantial land holdings were directly managed by the owning families in the early Ming period, but toward the end of the era marketing and ownership were depersonalized by the increased circulation of silver as money, and estate management gravitated into the hands of hired bailiffs. Together with the departure of the most talented youth into the imperial service, the result was direct contacts between the elite and subject groups were disrupted, and romantic images of country life disappeared from the literature.  In villages across China elite families participated in the life of the empire by sending their sons into the very high status imperial civil service. Most of the successful sons had a common education in the county and prefectural schools, had been recruited by competitive examination, and were posted to offices that might be anywhere in the empire, including the imperial capital. At first the recommendation of an elite local sponsor was important; increasing the imperial government relied more on merit exams, and thus entry into the national ruling class became more difficult. Downward social mobility into the peasantry was possible for less successful sons; upward mobility from the peasant class was unheard of.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Qing_dynasty:_1644.E2.80.931912"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Qing_dynasty:_1644–1912">Qing dynasty: 1644–1912</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Qing dynasty: 1644–1912">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manchu" title="Manchu">Manchus</a> (a tribe from Manchuria) conquered the <a href="/wiki/Shun_dynasty" title="Shun dynasty">Shun dynasty</a> (which was established after the Ming fell due to a peasant rebellion) around 1644–1683 in wars that killed perhaps 25 million people. The Manchus ruled it as the Qing dynasty until the early 20th century. Notably, Han men were forced to wear the long <a href="/wiki/Queue_(hairstyle)" title="Queue (hairstyle)">queue</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Pigtail" title="Pigtail">pigtail</a>)  as a mark of their inferior status. That said, some Han did achieve high rank in the civil service via the Imperial Examination system. Until the 19th century, Han immigration into Manchuria was forbidden. Chinese had an advanced artistic culture and well-developed science and technology.  However, its science and technology stood still after 1700 and in the 21st century very little survives outside museums and remote villages, except in for the ever-popular forms of traditional medicine like acupuncture. In the late Qing era (1900 to 1911), the country was beset by large-scale civil wars, major famines, military defeats by Britain and Japan, regional control by powerful warlords and foreign intervention such as the <a href="/wiki/Boxer_Rebellion" title="Boxer Rebellion">Boxer Rebellion</a> of 1900. Final collapse came in 1911.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_success_in_18th_century">Military success in 18th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Military success in 18th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ten_Great_Campaigns" title="Ten Great Campaigns">Ten Great Campaigns</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Qianlong_Emperor" title="Qianlong Emperor">Qianlong Emperor</a> from the 1750s to the 1790s <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty_in_Inner_Asia" title="Qing dynasty in Inner Asia">extended Qing control</a> into <a href="/wiki/Inner_Asia" title="Inner Asia">Inner Asia</a>. During the peak of the Qing dynasty, the empire ruled over the entirety of today's Mainland China, Hainan, <a href="/wiki/Taiwan_under_Qing_rule" title="Taiwan under Qing rule">Taiwan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mongolia_under_Qing_rule" title="Mongolia under Qing rule">Mongolia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Outer_Manchuria" title="Outer Manchuria">Outer Manchuria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northwest_China#Outer_Northwest_China" title="Northwest China">Outer Northwest China</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_defeats_in_19th_century">Military defeats in 19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Military defeats in 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Despite its origin in military conquest, and the long warlike tradition of the Manchu people who formed its ruling class, by the 19th century the Qing state was militarily extremely weak, poorly trained, lacking modern weapons and plagued by corruption and incompetence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>They repeatedly lost against the Western powers. Two Opium Wars (鸦片战争 yāpiàn zhànzhēng), pitted China against Western powers, notably Britain and France. China quickly lost both wars. After each defeat, the victors forced the Chinese government to make major concessions. After the first war 1839–1842, the treaty ceded Hong Kong island to Britain, and opened five "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_port" title="Treaty port">treaty ports</a>" including <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a>  and <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a> (Canton), and others of less importance <a href="/wiki/Xiamen" title="Xiamen">Xiamen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fuzhou" title="Fuzhou">Fuzhou</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ningbo" title="Ningbo">Ningbo</a>) to Western trade. After the second, Britain acquired Kowloon (the peninsula opposite Hong Kong island), and inland cities such as <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a> and Hangkou (now part of <a href="/wiki/Wuhan" title="Wuhan">Wuhan</a>) were opened to trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Defeat in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Opium_War" title="Second Opium War">Second Opium War</a>, 1856–1860, was utterly humiliating for China. The British and French sent ambassadors, escorted by a small army, to Beijing to see the treaty signed. The Emperor, however, did not receive ambassadors in anything like the Western sense; the closest Chinese expression translates as "tribute-bearer". To the Chinese court, Western envoys were just a group of new outsiders who should show appropriate respect for the emperor like any other visitors; of course the kowtow (knocking one's head on the floor) was a required part of the protocol. For that matter, the kowtow was required in dealing with any Chinese official. From the viewpoint of Western powers, treating China's decadent medieval regime with any respect at all was being generous. The envoy of Queen Victoria or another power might give some courtesies, even pretend for form's sake that the Emperor was the equal of their own ruler. However, they considered the notion that they should kowtow utterly ludicrous. In fact, it was official policy that no Briton of any rank should kowtow in any circumstances.
</p><p>China engaged in various stalling tactics to avoid actually signing the humiliating treaty to which their envoys had already agreed, and the scandalous possibility of an envoy coming before the Emperor and failing to kowtow. The ambassadors' progress to Beijing was impeded at every step. Several battles were fought, in each of which Chinese forces were soundly thrashed by numerically inferior Western forces. Eventually, Beijing was occupied, the treaty signed and embassies established. The British took the luxurious house of a Manchu general prominent in opposing their advance as their embassy.
</p><p>In retaliation for Chinese torture and murder of captives, including envoys taken while under a flag of truce, British and French forces also utterly destroyed the Yuan Ming Yuan (<a href="/wiki/Old_Summer_Palace" title="Old Summer Palace">Old Summer Palace</a>), an enormous complex of gardens and buildings outside Beijing. It took 3500 troops to loot it, wreck it and set it alight, and it burned for three days sending up a column of smoke clearly visible in Beijing. Once the Summer Palace was reduced to ruins a sign was raised with an inscription in Chinese stating "This is the reward for perfidy and cruelty". The choice to destroy the Palace was quite deliberate; they wanted something quite visible that struck at the upper classes who had ordered the crimes. Like the Forbidden City, no ordinary Chinese citizen had ever been allowed into the Summer Palace, as it was used exclusively by the Imperial family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1884–1885, China and France fought a war that resulted in China's accepting French control over their former tributary states in what is now Vietnam. The Qing armies acquitted themselves well in campaigns in Guangxi and Taiwan. However, the French sank much of China's modernized Fuzhou-based naval fleet in an afternoon.
</p><p>They also lost repeatedly against Japan, partly because Britain had helped modernise Japanese forces as a counter to Russian influence in the region. In 1879, Japan annexed the Ryukyu Kingdom, then a Chinese tributary state, and incorporated it as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Okinawa" title="Okinawa">Okinawa</a> prefecture. Despite pleas from a Ryukyuan envoy, China was powerless to send an army. The Chinese sought help from the British, who refused to intervene. In 1895, China lost the Sino-Japanese war and ceded Taiwan, the <a href="/wiki/Penghu" title="Penghu">Penghu</a> islands and the Liaodong peninsula to Japan. In addition, it had to relinquish control of Korea, which had been a tributary state of China for a long time.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rebellions">Rebellions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Rebellions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Qing also had internal troubles, notably several Muslim rebellions in the West and the Taiping Rebellion in the South, with millions dead and tens of millions more impoverished.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Taiping_Rebellion" title="Taiping Rebellion">Taiping Rebellion</a>, 1851–1864, was led by a charismatic figure claiming to be Christ's younger brother. It was largely a peasant revolt. The Taiping program included land reform and eliminating slavery, concubinage, arranged marriage, opium, footbinding, judicial torture and idolatry. The Qing government, with some Western help, eventually defeated the Taiping rebels, but not before they had ruled much of southern China for over ten years. This was one of the bloodiest wars ever fought; only World War II killed more people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Chinese resented much during this period — notably Christian missionaries, opium, annexation of Chinese land and the extraterritoriality that made foreigners immune to Chinese law. To the West, trade and missionaries were obviously good things, and extraterritoriality was necessary to protect their citizens from the corrupt Chinese system. To many Chinese, however, these were yet more examples of the West exploiting China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Boxer_Rebellion_1898.E2.80.931900"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Boxer_Rebellion_1898–1900">Boxer Rebellion 1898–1900</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Boxer Rebellion 1898–1900">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Boxer_Rebellion" title="Boxer Rebellion">Boxer Rebellion</a></div>
<p>Around 1898, these feelings exploded. The Boxers, also known as the "Society of Righteous and Harmonious Fists" (义和团 yì hé tuán) led a peasant religious/political movement whose main goal was to drive out evil foreign influences. Some believed their kung fu and prayer could stop bullets. While initially anti-Qing, once the revolt began they received some support from the Qing court and regional officials. The Boxers killed a few missionaries and many Chinese Christians, and eventually besieged the embassies in Beijing. An eight-nation alliance — Germany, France, Italy, Russia, Great Britain, the United States, Austria-Hungary and Japan — sent a force up from <a href="/wiki/Tianjin" title="Tianjin">Tianjin</a> to rescue the legations. The Qing had to accept foreign troops permanently posted in Beijing and pay a large indemnity as a result. In addition, Shanghai was divided among China and the eight nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Last_minute_reforms_1898.E2.80.931908"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_minute_reforms_1898–1908">Last minute reforms 1898–1908</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Last minute reforms 1898–1908">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Days%27_Reform" title="Hundred Days' Reform">Hundred Days' Reform</a></div>
<p>The <b>Hundred Days' Reform</b> was a failed 103-day national, cultural, political, and educational reform movement in 1898.  It was undertaken by the young <a href="/wiki/Guangxu_Emperor" title="Guangxu Emperor">Guangxu Emperor</a> and his reform-minded supporters. Following the issuing of the reformative edicts, a <i><a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d'état">coup d'état</a></i> ("The Coup of 1898", <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wuxu_Coup" title="Wuxu Coup">Wuxu Coup</a>) was perpetrated by powerful conservative opponents led by <a href="/wiki/Empress_Dowager_Cixi" title="Empress Dowager Cixi">Empress Dowager Cixi</a>, who became a virtual dictator.
</p><p>The Boxer Rebellion was a humiliating fiasco for China: the Qing rulers proved visibly incompetent and lost prestige irreparably, while the foreign powers gained greater influence in Chinese affairs. The humiliation stimulated a second reform movement—this time sanctioned by the empress dowager Cixi herself. From 1901 to 1908, the dynasty announced a series of educational, military, and administrative reforms, many reminiscent of the "one hundreds days" of 1898. In 1905 the examination system itself was abolished and the entire Confucian tradition of merit entry into the elite collapsed. The abolition of the traditional civil service examination was itself a revolution of immense significance. After many centuries, the scholar's mind began to be liberated from the shackles of classical studies, and social mobility no longer depended chiefly on the writing of stereotyped and flowery prose. New ministries were created in Beijing and revised law codes were drafted. Work began on a national budget—the national government had no idea how much taxes were collected in its name and spent by regional officials. New armies were raised and trained in European (and Japanese) fashion and plans for a national army were laid.  The creation of the "new army" reflected rising esteem for the military profession and the emergence of a new national elite that dominated China for much of the 20th century. . More officers and men were now literate, while patriotism and better pay served as an inducement for service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reform_and_revolution">Reform and revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Reform and revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The movement for constitutionalism gathered momentum following the Russo-Japanese War of 1904–1905, for Japan's victory signalled the triumph of constitutionalism over absolutism.  Under pressure from gentry and student groups, the Qing court in 1908 issued plans for the inauguration of consultative provincial assemblies in 1909, a consultative national assembly in 1910, and both a constitution and a parliament in 1917. The consultative assemblies were to play a pivotal role in the unfolding events, politicizing the provincial gentry and providing them with new leverage with which to protect their interests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ironically, the measures designed to preserve the Qing dynasty hastened its death, for the nationalistic and modernizing impulses generated or nurtured by the reforms brought a greater awareness of the Qing government's extreme backwardness. Modernizing forces emerged as business, students, women, soldiers, and overseas Chinese became mobilized and demanded change.  Government-sponsored education in Japan, available to both civilian and military students, exposed Chinese youths to revolutionary ideas produced by political exiles and inspired by the West. Anti-Manchu revolutionary groups were formed in the Yangtze cities by 1903, and those in Tokyo banded together to form the "Revolutionary Alliance" in 1905, led by <a href="/wiki/Sun_Yat-sen" title="Sun Yat-sen">Sun Yat-sen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Joseon_Korea:_1392.E2.80.931897"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Joseon_Korea:_1392–1897">Joseon Korea: 1392–1897</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Joseon Korea: 1392–1897">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Joseon" title="Joseon">Joseon</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Danwon-Huwonyuyeon.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1777" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="382" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Danwon-Huwonyuyeon.JPG/220px-Danwon-Huwonyuyeon.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Danwon-Huwonyuyeon.JPG/330px-Danwon-Huwonyuyeon.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Danwon-Huwonyuyeon.JPG/440px-Danwon-Huwonyuyeon.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Danwon-Huwonyuyeon.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Korean people in a painting from the 18th century</div></div></div>
<p>In July 1392, General <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yi_Seong-gye" title="Yi Seong-gye">Yi Seong-gye</a> overthrew the Goryeo dynasty and founded a new dynasty, Joseon. As King <a href="/wiki/Taejo_of_Joseon" title="Taejo of Joseon">Taejo of Joseon</a>, he chose Hanyang (<a href="/wiki/Seoul" title="Seoul">Seoul</a>) as the capital of the new dynasty. During its 500-year reign, Joseon encouraged the entrenchment of Confucian ideals and doctrines in Korean society. <a href="/wiki/Neo-Confucianism" title="Neo-Confucianism">Neo-Confucianism</a> was installed as the new dynasty's state ideology. Joseon consolidated its effective rule over the territory of current Korea and saw the height of classical Korean culture, trade, literature, and science and technology. Joseon dynasty was a highly centralized monarchy and neo-Confucian bureaucracy as codified by <i><a href="/wiki/Gyeongguk_daejeon" title="Gyeongguk daejeon">Gyeongguk daejeon</a></i>, a sort of Joseon constitution. The king had absolute authority, the officials were also expected to persuade the king to the right path if the latter was thought to be mistaken. He was bound by tradition, precedents set by earlier kings, Gyeongguk daejeon, and Confucian teachings. In theory, there were three social classes, but in practice, there were four. The top class were the <a href="/wiki/Yangban" title="Yangban">yangban</a>, or "scholar-<a href="/wiki/Gentry" title="Gentry">gentry</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENahm1996100–102_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENahm1996100–102-67">[67]</a></sup> the commoners were called <a href="/wiki/Sangmin" title="Sangmin">sangmin</a> or <i>yangmin</i>, and the lowest class was that of the <a href="/wiki/Cheonmin" title="Cheonmin">cheonmin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeth2010165–167_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeth2010165–167-68">[68]</a></sup> Between the yangban and the commoners was a fourth class, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chungin" title="Chungin">chungin</a>, "middle people".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeth2010170_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeth2010170-69">[69]</a></sup> Joseon Korea installed a centralised administrative system controlled by civil bureaucrats and military officers who were collectively called Yangban. Yangban strove to do well at the royal examinations to obtain high positions in the government. They had to excel in calligraphy, poetry, classical Chinese texts, and Confucian rites. In order to become an official, one had to pass a series of <a href="/wiki/Gwageo" title="Gwageo">gwageo</a> examinations. There were three kinds of gwageo exams - literary, military, and miscellaneous.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Edo_Japan">Edo Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Edo Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo period</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Edo_l245.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Edo_l245.jpg/220px-Edo_l245.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Edo_l245.jpg/330px-Edo_l245.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Edo_l245.jpg/440px-Edo_l245.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Edo_l245.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>View of <a href="/wiki/Edo" title="Edo">Edo</a>, from a 17th-century screen painting</div></div></div>
<p>In 1603, the <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_shogunate" title="Tokugawa shogunate">Tokugawa shogunate</a> (military dictatorship) ushered in a long period of isolation from foreign influence in order to secure its power. For 250 years this policy enabled Japan to enjoy stability and a flowering of its indigenous culture. Early modern Japanese society had an elaborate social structure, in which everyone knew their place and level of prestige.  At the top were the emperor and the court nobility, invincible in prestige but weak in power. Next came the "bushi" of shōgun, daimyō and layers of feudal lords whose rank was indicated by their closeness to the Tokugawa. They had power. The "daimyō" were about 250 local lords of local "han" with annual outputs of 50,000 or more bushels of rice. The upper strata was much given to elaborate and expensive rituals, including elegant architecture, landscaped gardens, nō drama, patronage of the arts, and the tea ceremony.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Three_cultures">Three cultures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Three cultures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Three distinct cultural traditions operated during the Tokugawa era, having little to do with each other.  In the villages the peasants had their own rituals and localistic traditions.  In the high society of the imperial court, daimyō and samurai, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_influence_on_Japanese_culture" title="Chinese influence on Japanese culture">Chinese cultural influence</a> was paramount, especially in the areas of ethics and political ideals.  Neo-Confucianism became the approved philosophy, and was taught in official schools; Confucian norms regarding personal duty and family honor became deeply implanted in elite thought. Equally pervasive was the Chinese influence in painting, decorative arts and history, economics, and natural science. One exception came in religion, where there was a revival of <a href="/wiki/Shinto" title="Shinto">Shinto</a>, which had originated in Japan. Motoori Norinaga (1730–1801) freed Shinto from centuries of Buddhist accretions and gave a new emphasis to the myth of imperial divine descent, which later became a political tool for imperialist conquest until it was destroyed in 1945. The third cultural level was the popular art of the low-status artisans, merchants and entertainers, especially in Edo and other cities. It revolved around "ukiyo", the floating world of the city pleasure quarters and theaters that was officially off-limits to samurai. Its actors and courtesans were favorite subjects of the woodblock color prints that reached high levels of technical and artistic achievement in the 18th century. They also appeared in the novels and short stories of popular prose writers of the age like Ihara Saikaku (1642–1693). The theater itself, both in the puppet drama and the newer kabuki, as written by the greatest dramatist, <a href="/wiki/Chikamatsu_Monzaemon" title="Chikamatsu Monzaemon">Chikamatsu Monzaemon</a> (1653–1724), relied on the clash between duty and inclination in the context of revenge and love.
</p>
<h4><span id="Growth_of_Edo.2FTokyo"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Growth_of_Edo/Tokyo">Growth of Edo/Tokyo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Growth of Edo/Tokyo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Edo (Tokyo) had been a small settlement for 400 years but began to grow rapidly after 1603 when Shōgun <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ieyasu" title="Ieyasu">Ieyasu</a> built a fortified city as the administrative center of the new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tokugawa_Shogunate" title="Tokugawa Shogunate">Tokugawa Shogunate</a>. Edo resembled the capital cities of Europe with military, political, and economic functions. The Tokugawa political system rested on both feudal and bureaucratic controls, so that Edo lacked a unitary administration. The typical urban social order was composed of samurai, unskilled workers and servants, artisans, and businessmen. The artisans and businessmen were organized in officially sanctioned guilds; their numbers grew rapidly as Tokyo grew and became a national trading center. Businessmen were excluded from government office, and in response they created their own subculture of entertainment, making Edo a cultural as well as a political and economic center. With the Meiji Restoration, Tokyo's political, economic, and cultural functions simply continued as the new capital of imperial Japan.
</p>
<h2><span id="1750.E2.80.931900_Western_Colonialism"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1750–1900_Western_Colonialism">1750–1900 Western Colonialism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: 1750–1900 Western Colonialism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:British_ships_in_Canton.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1383" decoding="async" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/British_ships_in_Canton.jpg/220px-British_ships_in_Canton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/British_ships_in_Canton.jpg/330px-British_ships_in_Canton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/British_ships_in_Canton.jpg/440px-British_ships_in_Canton.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:British_ships_in_Canton.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British assault on <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Canton</a> during the <a href="/wiki/First_Opium_War" title="First Opium War">First Opium War</a>, May 1841</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Meiji_Era">The Meiji Era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: The Meiji Era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Kanagawa" title="Treaty of Kanagawa">Treaty of Kanagawa</a> with the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States of America</a> in 1854, Japan opened its ports and began to intensively modernise and industrialise. The <a href="/wiki/Meiji_Restoration" title="Meiji Restoration">Meiji Restoration</a> of 1868 ended the Tokugawa period, and put Japan on a course of centralized modern government in the name of the Emperor.  During late nineteenth and early twentieth century, Japan became a regional power that was able to defeat the militaries of both China and <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>. It occupied <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Formosa" title="Formosa">Formosa</a> (<a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>), and southern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sakhalin_Island" title="Sakhalin Island">Sakhalin Island</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pacific_War">Pacific War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Pacific War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1931 Japan occupied <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a> ("Dongbei") after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manchurian_Incident" title="Manchurian Incident">Manchurian Incident</a>, and in 1937 it launched a <a href="/wiki/Second_Sino-Japanese_War" title="Second Sino-Japanese War">full-scale invasion of China</a>. The U.S. undertook large scale military and economic aid to China and demanded Japanese withdrawal.  Instead of withdrawing, Japan invaded <a href="/wiki/French_Indochina" title="French Indochina">French Indochina</a> in 1940–41. In response, the U.S., Britain and the Netherlands cut off oil imports in 1941, which accounted for over 90% of Japan's oil supply. <a href="/wiki/Events_leading_to_the_attack_on_Pearl_Harbor" title="Events leading to the attack on Pearl Harbor">Negotiations with the US</a> led nowhere. Japan attacked U.S. forces at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Pearl_Harbor" title="Battle of Pearl Harbor">Battle of Pearl Harbor</a> in December 1941, triggering America's entry into <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. Japan rapidly expanded at sea and land, capturing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malayan_Campaign" title="Malayan Campaign">Singapore</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_invasion_of_the_Philippines" title="Japanese invasion of the Philippines">Philippines</a> in early 1942, and threatening India and Australia.
</p><p>Although it was to be a long and bloody war, Japan began to lose the initiative in 1942. At the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Coral_Sea" title="Battle of the Coral Sea">Battle of the Coral Sea</a>, a Japanese offensive was turned back, for the first time, at sea. The June <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Midway" title="Battle of Midway">Battle of Midway</a> cost Japan four of its six large <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carriers</a> and destroyed its capability for future major offensives. In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guadalcanal_Campaign" title="Guadalcanal Campaign">Guadalcanal Campaign</a>, the U.S. took back ground from Japan.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._occupation_of_Japan">U.S. occupation of Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: U.S. occupation of Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>After its defeat in World War II, Japan was occupied by the U.S. until 1951, and recovered from the effects of the war to become an economic power, staunch American ally and a liberal democracy. While Emperor Hirohito was allowed to retain his throne as a symbol of national unity, actual power rests in networks of powerful politicians, bureaucrats, and business executives.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cold_War">Cold War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Cold War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Japanese growth in the postwar period was often called a "miracle". It was led by manufacturing; starting with textiles and clothing and moving to high-technology, especially automobiles, electronics and computers. The economy experienced a major slowdown starting in the 1990s following three decades of unprecedented growth, but Japan still remains a major global economic power.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Civil_War" title="Chinese Civil War">Chinese Civil War</a> resumed after World War II concluded. In 1949, <a href="/wiki/Mao_Zedong" title="Mao Zedong">Mao Zedong</a> proclaimed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">People's Republic of China</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China_(1912%E2%80%931949)" title="Republic of China (1912–1949)">Republic of China</a>, which had governed <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">mainland China</a> until this point, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retreat_of_the_Republic_of_China_to_Taiwan" title="Retreat of the Republic of China to Taiwan">retreated</a> to <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>. Since then, the jurisdiction of the Republic of China has been limited to the <a href="/wiki/Free_area_of_the_Republic_of_China" title="Free area of the Republic of China">Taiwan Area</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-China1_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-China1-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-China2_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-China2-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the surrender of Japan, at the end of World War II, on 15 August (officially 2 September) 1945, <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Korea" title="Division of Korea">Korea was divided</a> at the <a href="/wiki/38th_parallel_north" title="38th parallel north">38th parallel</a> into two zones of occupation. The Soviets administered the northern-half and the Americans administered the southern-half. In 1948, as a result of <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> tensions, the occupation zones became two sovereign states. This led to the establishment of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_Korea" title="Republic of Korea">Republic of Korea</a> in South Korea on 15 August 1948, promptly followed by the establishment of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Democratic_People%27s_Republic_of_Korea" title="Democratic People's Republic of Korea">Democratic People's Republic of Korea</a> in North Korea on 9 September 1948. In 1950, after years of mutual hostilities, North Korea invaded South Korea in an attempt to re-unify the peninsula under its communist rule. The subsequent <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>, which lasted from 1950 to 1953, ended with a stalemate and has left the two Koreas separated by the <a href="/wiki/Korean_Demilitarized_Zone" title="Korean Demilitarized Zone">Korean Demilitarized Zone</a> (DMZ) up to the present day.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_of_Religion">Decline of Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Decline of Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Historically, cultures and regions strongly influenced by Confucianism include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Culture_of_China" title="Culture of China">Mainland China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Hong_Kong" title="Culture of Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Macau" title="Culture of Macau">Macau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Taiwan" title="Culture of Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Japan" title="Culture of Japan">Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_North_Korea" title="Culture of North Korea">North Korea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Korea" title="Culture of Korea">South Korea</a>, as well as territories settled predominantly by <a href="/wiki/Overseas_Chinese" title="Overseas Chinese">Overseas Chinese</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Singapore" title="Culture of Singapore">Singapore</a>. The abolition of the examination system in 1905 marked the end of official Confucianism. The <a href="/wiki/New_Culture_Movement" title="New Culture Movement">New culture</a> intellectuals of the early twentieth century blamed Confucianism for China's weaknesses. They searched for new doctrines to replace Confucianism, some of these new ideologies include the "<a href="/wiki/Three_Principles_of_the_People" title="Three Principles of the People">Three Principles of the People</a>" with the establishment of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_China_(1912-1949)" title="Republic of China (1912-1949)">Republic of China</a>, and then <a href="/wiki/Maoism" title="Maoism">Maoism</a> under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">People's Republic of China</a>.
</p><p>In Japan, the presence of a liberal order and consumerism led to a voluntarily decline of religious belief.
</p><p>Around the turn of the 21st centuries there were talks of a "Confucian Revival" in the academia and the scholarly community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Across the region cultural institutions of religions have remained, even while actual belief has declined.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Maps">Maps</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_East_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Maps">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-43">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFWatson2006">Watson, Peter (2006). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=CVNoJydnGAoC&amp;pg=PA304" rel="nofollow"><i>Ideas: A History of Thought and Invention, from Fire to Freud</i></a>. HarperCollins. p. 304. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-06-093564-1" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-06-093564-1"><bdi>978-0-06-093564-1</bdi></a>. <q>The first use of a metal tube in this context was made around 1280 in the wars between the Song and the Mongols, where a new term, chong, was invented to describe the new horror...Like paper, it reached the West via the Muslims, in this case the writings of the Andalusian botanist Ibn al-Baytar, who died in Damascus in 1248. The Arabic term for saltpetre is 'Chinese snow' while the Persian usage is 'Chinese salt'.28</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Ideas%3A+A+History+of+Thought+and+Invention%2C+from+Fire+to+Freud&amp;rft.pages=304&amp;rft.pub=HarperCollins&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-06-093564-1&amp;rft.aulast=Watson&amp;rft.aufirst=Peter&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DCVNoJydnGAoC%26pg%3DPA304&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHistory+of+East+Asia"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-44">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1 cs1-prop-long-vol" id="CITEREFCathal_J._Nolan2006">Cathal J. Nolan (2006). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=1h9zzSH-NmwC&amp;pg=PA365" rel="nofollow"><i>The age of wars of religion, 1000–1650: an encyclopedia of global warfare and civilization</i></a>. Vol. 1 of Greenwood encyclopedias of modern world wars. Greenwood Publishing Group. p. 365. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-313-33733-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-313-33733-8"><bdi>978-0-313-33733-8</bdi></a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2011-11-28</span></span>. <q>In either case, there is linguistic evidence of Chinese origins of the technology: in Damascus, Arabs called the saltpeter used in making gunpowder " Chinese snow," while in Iran it was called "Chinese salt." Whatever the migratory route</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+age+of+wars+of+religion%2C+1000%E2%80%931650%3A+an+encyclopedia+of+global+warfare+and+civilization&amp;rft.pages=365&amp;rft.pub=Greenwood+Publishing+Group&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-313-33733-8&amp;rft.au=Cathal+J.+Nolan&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D1h9zzSH-NmwC%26pg%3DPA365&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHistory+of+East+Asia"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-45">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFOliver_Frederick_Gillilan_Hogg1970">Oliver Frederick Gillilan Hogg (1970). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=uY_fAAAAMAAJ" rel="nofollow"><i>Artillery: its origin, heyday, and decline</i></a>. <li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFPartington1960">Partington, J.R.
